            </i>
            <br/>

            </small>
           </li>
           <li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0">

            <i>

            </i>
            <br/>
            <small>

            </small>
            <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dodd2_5-0">
             <a href="#cite_note-dodd2-5">
              <span>

              </span>

              <span>

              </span>
             </a>
            </sup>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       loggerhead sea turtle
      </b>
      (
      <i>
       Caretta caretta
      </i>
      ), or
      <b>
       loggerhead
      </b>
      , is an
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">
       oceanic turtle
      </a>
      distributed throughout the world. It is a marine reptile, belonging to the family
      <a href="/wiki/Cheloniidae" title="Cheloniidae">
       Cheloniidae
      </a>
      . The average loggerhead measures around 90cm (35in) long when fully grown, although larger specimens of up to 280cm (110in) have been discovered. The adult loggerhead sea turtle weighs approximately 135kg (298lb), with the largest specimens weighing in at more than 450kg (1,000lb). The skin ranges from yellow to brown in color, and the shell is typically reddish-brown. No external differences in gender are seen until the turtle becomes an adult, the most obvious difference being the adult males have thicker tails and shorter
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastron" title="Plastron">
       plastrons
      </a>
      than the females.
     </p>
     <p>
      The loggerhead sea turtle is found in the
      <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">
       Atlantic
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">
       Pacific
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">
       Indian Oceans
      </a>
      , as well as the
      <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">
       Mediterranean Sea
      </a>
      . It spends most of its life in saltwater and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estuarine" title="Estuarine">
       estuarine
      </a>
      habitats, with females briefly coming ashore to lay eggs. The loggerhead sea turtle has a low reproductive rate; females lay an average of four egg
      <a href="/wiki/Clutch_(eggs)" title="Clutch (eggs)">
       clutches
      </a>
      and then become
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/quiescence" title="wiktionary:quiescence">
       quiescent
      </a>
      , producing no eggs for two to three years. The loggerhead reaches sexual maturity within 1733 years and has a lifespan of 4767 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-History-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The loggerhead sea turtle is
      <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">
       omnivorous
      </a>
      , feeding mainly on bottom-dwelling
      <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">
       invertebrates
      </a>
      . Its large and powerful jaws serve as an effective tool for dismantling its prey. Young loggerheads are exploited by numerous predators; the eggs are especially vulnerable to terrestrial organisms. Once the turtles reach adulthood, their formidable size limits predation to large marine animals, such as sharks.
     </p>
     <p>
      Loggerheads are considered an
      <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">
       endangered species
      </a>
      and are protected by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Union_for_the_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for the Conservation of Nature">
       International Union for the Conservation of Nature
      </a>
      . Untended fishing gear is responsible for many loggerhead deaths. Turtles may also suffocate if they are trapped in
      <a href="/wiki/Trawling" title="Trawling">
       fishing trawls
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Turtle_excluder_device" title="Turtle excluder device">
       Turtle excluder devices
      </a>
      have been implemented in efforts to reduce mortality by providing an escape route for the turtles. Loss of suitable nesting beaches and the introduction of exotic predators have also taken a toll on loggerhead populations. Efforts to restore their numbers will require international cooperation, since the turtles roam vast areas of ocean and critical nesting beaches are scattered across several countries.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Description">
       Description
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_turtle.jpg">
        <img alt="Photo of the carapace of a loggerhead sea turtle." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Loggerhead_turtle.jpg/220px-Loggerhead_turtle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Loggerhead_turtle.jpg/330px-Loggerhead_turtle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Loggerhead_turtle.jpg/440px-Loggerhead_turtle.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_turtle.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The carapace of this loggerhead is reddish-brown; five vertebral scutes run down the turtle's midline bordered by five pairs of costal scutes.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The loggerhead sea turtle is the world's second largest hard-shelled turtle.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst37_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst37-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Adults have an average weight range of 80 to 200kg (180 to 440lb) and a length range of 70 to 95cm (28 to 37in).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst37_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst37-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The maximum reported weight is 545kg (1,202lb) and the maximum carapace length is 213cm (84in).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst37_7-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst37-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The head and
      <a href="/wiki/Carapace" title="Carapace">
       carapace
      </a>
      (upper shell) range from a yellow-orange to a reddish-brown, while the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastron" title="Plastron">
       plastron
      </a>
      (underside) is typically pale yellow.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtleguide104_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-turtleguide104-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The turtle's neck and sides are brown on the tops and yellow on the sides and bottom.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-noaa-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The turtle's shell is divided into two sections: carapace and plastron. The carapace is further divided into large plates, or
      <a href="/wiki/Scute" title="Scute">
       scutes
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtleguide104_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-turtleguide104-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Typically, 11 or 12 pairs of marginal scutes rim the carapace.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seaturtlebook7_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-seaturtlebook7-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Five vertebral scutes run down the carapace's midline, while five pairs of costal scutes border them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtleguide110_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-turtleguide110-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nuchal" title="wikt:nuchal">
       nuchal
      </a>
      scute is located at the base of the head.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtleguide110_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-turtleguide110-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The carapace connects to the plastron by three pairs of inframarginal scutes forming the bridge of the shell.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtleguide110_11-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-turtleguide110-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The plastron features paired
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gular_scute" title="Gular scute">
       gular
      </a>
      , humeral, pectoral, abdominal, femoral, and anal scutes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seaturtlebook7_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-seaturtlebook7-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The shell serves as external armor, although loggerhead sea turtles cannot retract their heads or flippers into their shells.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anatomy_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-anatomy-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">
       Sexual dimorphism
      </a>
      of the loggerhead sea turtle is only apparent in adults. Adult males have longer tails and claws than females. The males' plastrons are shorter than the females', presumably to accommodate the males' larger tails. The carapaces of males are wider and less domed than the females', and males typically have wider heads than females.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-valente22_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-valente22-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The sex of juveniles and subadults cannot be determined through external anatomy, but can be observed through dissection,
      <a href="/wiki/Laparoscopy" title="Laparoscopy">
       laparoscopy
      </a>
      (an operation performed on the abdomen),
      <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">
       histological
      </a>
      examination (cell anatomy), and radioimmunological assays (immune study dealing with
      <a href="/wiki/Isotopic_labeling" title="Isotopic labeling">
       radiolabeling
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-valente22_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-valente22-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lachrymal_glands" title="Lachrymal glands">
       Lachrymal glands
      </a>
      located behind each eye allow the loggerhead to maintain
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osmotic_balance" title="Osmotic balance">
       osmotic balance
      </a>
      by eliminating the excess salt obtained from ingesting ocean water. On land, the excretion of excess salt gives the false impression that the turtle is crying.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peaker231_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-peaker231-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">
       Distribution
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Distribution">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_turtle.png">
        <img alt="A map of the range of a loggerhead sea turtle covering the Atlantic, Pacific, and Indian Oceans, and the Mediterranean Sea" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="636" data-file-width="1204" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Loggerhead_turtle.png/300px-Loggerhead_turtle.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Loggerhead_turtle.png/450px-Loggerhead_turtle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Loggerhead_turtle.png/600px-Loggerhead_turtle.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_turtle.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Range of the loggerhead sea turtle
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The loggerhead sea turtle has a
      <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitan_distribution" title="Cosmopolitan distribution">
       cosmopolitan distribution
      </a>
      , nesting over the broadest geographical range of any sea turtle. It inhabits the Atlantic, Indian, and Pacific Oceans and the Mediterranean Sea.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila164_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila164-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the Atlantic Ocean, the greatest concentration of loggerheads is along the southeastern coast of North America and in the Gulf of Mexico. Very few loggerheads are found along the European and African coastlines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila165_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila165-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">
       Florida
      </a>
      is the most popular nesting site, with more than 67,000 nests built per year. Nesting extends as far north as
      <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">
       Virginia
      </a>
      , as far south as Brazil, and as far east as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Verde_Islands" title="Cape Verde Islands">
       Cape Verde Islands
      </a>
      . The Cape Verde Islands are the only significant nesting site on the eastern side of the Atlantic. Loggerheads found in the Atlantic Ocean feed from Canada to Brazil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila164_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila164-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the Indian Ocean, loggerheads feed along the coastlines of Africa, the
      <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">
       Arabian Peninsula
      </a>
      , and in the
      <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">
       Arabian Sea
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila166_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila166-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Along the African coastline, loggerheads nest from
      <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">
       Mozambique
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Bazaruto_Archipelago" title="Bazaruto Archipelago">
       Bazaruto Archipelago
      </a>
      to South Africa's
      <a href="/wiki/ISimangaliso_Wetland_Park" title="ISimangaliso Wetland Park">
       St Lucia
      </a>
      estuary.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seaturtlebook8_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-seaturtlebook8-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The largest Indian Ocean nesting site is
      <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">
       Oman
      </a>
      , on the Arabian Peninsula, which hosts around 15,000 nests, giving it the second largest nesting population of loggerheads in the world.
      <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">
       Western Australia
      </a>
      is another notable nesting area, with 1,0002,000 nests per year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila166_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila166-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Pacific loggerheads live in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">
       temperate
      </a>
      to tropical regions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seaturtlebook8_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-seaturtlebook8-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They forage in the
      <a href="/wiki/East_China_Sea" title="East China Sea">
       East China Sea
      </a>
      , the southwestern Pacific, and along the
      <a href="/wiki/Baja_California_Peninsula" title="Baja California Peninsula">
       Baja California Peninsula
      </a>
      . Eastern Australia and Japan are the major nesting areas, with the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">
       Great Barrier Reef
      </a>
      deemed an important nesting area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pacific loggerheads occasionally nest in
      <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">
       Vanuatu
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tokelau" title="Tokelau">
       Tokelau
      </a>
      .
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yakushima_Island" title="Yakushima Island">
       Yakushima Island
      </a>
      is the most important site, with three nesting grounds visited by 40% of all nearby loggerheads.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila166_17-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila166-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After nesting, females often find homes in the East China Sea, while the
      <a href="/wiki/Kuroshio_Current" title="Kuroshio Current">
       Kuroshio Current Extension's
      </a>
      Bifurcation region provides important juvenile foraging areas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seaturtlebook8_18-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-seaturtlebook8-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Eastern Pacific populations are concentrated off the coast of Baja California, where upwelling provides rich feeding grounds for juvenile turtles and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subadult" title="Subadult">
       subadults
      </a>
      . Nesting sites along the eastern Pacific Basin are rare. mtDNA sequence polymorphism analysis and tracking studies suggest 95% of the population along the coast of the Americas hatch on the Japanese Islands in the western Pacific.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen3731_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bowen3731-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The turtles are transported by the prevailing currents across the full length of the northern Pacific, one of the longest migration routes of any marine animal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen3731_20-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bowen3731-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The return journey to the natal beaches in Japan has been long suspected, although the trip would cross unproductive clear water with few feeding opportunities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen3733_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bowen3733-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Evidence of a return journey came from an adult female loggerhead named Adelita, which in 1996, equipped with a satellite tracking device, made the 14500-km (9000-mi) trip from Mexico across the Pacific. Adelita was the first animal of any kind ever tracked across an ocean basin.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBSNature_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PBSNature-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Mediterranean Sea is a nursery for juveniles, as well as a common place for adults in the spring and summer months.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila165_16-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila165-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seaturtlebook20_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-seaturtlebook20-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Almost 45% of the Mediterranean juvenile population has migrated from the Atlantic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila165_16-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila165-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Loggerheads feed in the
      <a href="/wiki/Alboran_Sea" title="Alboran Sea">
       Alboran Sea
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">
       Adriatic Sea
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila165_16-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila165-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Greece is the most popular nesting site along the Mediterranean, with more than 3,000 nests per year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila166_17-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila166-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because of this, Greek authorities do not allow planes to take off or land at night in
      <a href="/wiki/Zakynthos" title="Zakynthos">
       Zakynthos
      </a>
      due to the nesting turtles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flighttimes_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-flighttimes-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition to the Greek coast, the coastlines of Cyprus and Turkey are also common nesting sites.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila166_17-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila166-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One record of this turtle was made in Ireland washed ashore on Ballyhealy Beach in Co. Wexford.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">
       Habitat
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Habitat">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Loggerhead sea turtles spend most of their lives in the open ocean and in shallow coastal waters. They rarely come ashore, except for the females' brief visits to construct nests and deposit eggs. Hatchling loggerhead turtles live in floating mats of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sargassum" title="Sargassum">
        Sargassum
       </a>
      </i>
      algae.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila172_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila172-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Adults and juveniles live along the continental shelf, as well as in shallow coastal estuaries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila174_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila174-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the northwestern Atlantic Ocean, age plays a factor in habitat preference. Juveniles are more frequently found in shallow estuarine habitats with limited ocean access compared to non-nesting adults.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seaturtlebook11_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-seaturtlebook11-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Loggerheads occupy waters with surface temperatures ranging from 13.3-28.0C (56-82F) during non-nesting season. Temperatures from 27-28C are most suitable for nesting females.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst39_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst39-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Juvenile loggerheads share the
      <i>
       Sargassum
      </i>
      habitat with a variety of other organisms. The mats of
      <i>
       Sargassum
      </i>
      contain as many as 100 different species of animals on which the juveniles feed. Some of the prey, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">
       ants
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">
       flies
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">
       aphids
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Leafhopper" title="Leafhopper">
       leafhoppers
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">
       beetles
      </a>
      , are carried by the wind to these areas.
      <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">
       Endemic
      </a>
      prey of the mats include
      <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">
       barnacles
      </a>
      , small crab larvae,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_eggs" title="Fish eggs">
       fish eggs
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrozoa" title="Hydrozoa">
       hydrozoan
      </a>
      colonies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila172_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila172-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Marine mammals and commercial fish, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">
       tuna
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dolphin_fish" title="Dolphin fish">
       dolphin fish
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Amberjack" title="Amberjack">
       amberjacks
      </a>
      , also inhabit the mats.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sargassum_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sargassum-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology_and_behavior">
       Ecology and behavior
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ecology and behavior">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_Sea_Turtle_(Caretta_caretta)_2.jpg">
        <img alt="A loggerhead sea turtle resting under a rock with its eyes open" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1422" data-file-width="2169" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Loggerhead_Sea_Turtle_%28Caretta_caretta%29_2.jpg/220px-Loggerhead_Sea_Turtle_%28Caretta_caretta%29_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Loggerhead_Sea_Turtle_%28Caretta_caretta%29_2.jpg/330px-Loggerhead_Sea_Turtle_%28Caretta_caretta%29_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Loggerhead_Sea_Turtle_%28Caretta_caretta%29_2.jpg/440px-Loggerhead_Sea_Turtle_%28Caretta_caretta%29_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_Sea_Turtle_(Caretta_caretta)_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A resting loggerhead sea turtle
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Loggerhead sea turtles observed in captivity and in the wild are most active during the day. In captivity, the loggerheads' daily activities are divided between swimming and resting on the bottom. While resting, they spread their forelimbs to about midstroke swimming position. They remain motionless with eyes open or half-shut and are easily alerted during this state. At night, captives sleep in the same position with their eyes tightly shut, and are slow to react.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst39_29-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst39-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Loggerheads spend up to 85% of their day submerged, with males being the more active divers than females. The average duration of dives is 1530 min, but they can stay submerged for up to four hours.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst44_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst44-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Juvenile loggerheads and adults differ in their swimming methods. A juvenile keeps its forelimbs pressed to the side of its carapace, and propels itself by kicking with its hind limbs. As the juvenile matures, its swimming method is progressively replaced with the adult's alternating-limb method. They depend entirely on this method of swimming by one year old.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst43_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst43-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Water temperature affects the sea turtle's metabolic rate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst39_29-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst39-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" title="Fatigue (medical)">
       Lethargy
      </a>
      is induced at temperatures between 13 and 15C (55 and 59F). The loggerhead takes on a floating, cold-stunned posture when temperatures drop to around 10C.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst39_29-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst39-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, younger loggerheads are more resistant to cold and do not become stunned until temperatures drop below 9C. The loggerheads'
      <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">
       migration
      </a>
      helps to prevent instances of cold-stunning.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst40_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst40-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Higher water temperatures cause an increase in metabolism and heart rate. A loggerhead's body temperature increases in warmer waters more quickly than it decreases in colder water; their
      <a href="/wiki/Critical_thermal_maximum" title="Critical thermal maximum">
       critical thermal maximum
      </a>
      is currently unknown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst40_33-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst40-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Female-female aggression, which is fairly rare in other marine
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">
       vertebrates
      </a>
      , is common among loggerheads. Ritualized aggression escalates from passive threat displays to combat. This conflict primarily occurs over access to feeding grounds. Escalation typically follows four steps.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aggression_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-aggression-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      First, initial contact is stimulated by visual or tactile cues. Second, confrontation occurs, beginning with passive confrontations characterized by wide head-tail circling. They begin aggressive confrontation when one turtle ceases to circle and directly faces the other. Third,
      <a href="/wiki/Sparring" title="Sparring">
       sparring
      </a>
      occurs with turtles snapping at each others jaws. The final stage, separation, is either mutual, with both turtles swimming away in opposite directions, or involves chasing one out of the immediate vicinity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aggression_34-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-aggression-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Escalation is determined by several factors, including
      <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">
       hormone
      </a>
      levels, energy expenditure, expected outcome, and importance of location. At all stages, an upright tail shows willingness to escalate, while a curled tail shows willingness to submit. Because higher aggression is metabolically costly and potentially debilitating, contact is much more likely to escalate when the conflict is over access to good foraging grounds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aggression_34-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-aggression-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Further aggression has also been reported in captive loggerheads. The turtles are seemingly
      <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">
       territorial
      </a>
      , and will fight with other loggerheads and sea turtles of different species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst44_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst44-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">
       Feeding
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Feeding">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moon_jelly_-_adult_(rev2).jpg">
        <img alt="Translucent moon jelly on black blackground: The jelly contains a solid white mass extending through about two-thirds of its body" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Moon_jelly_-_adult_%28rev2%29.jpg/220px-Moon_jelly_-_adult_%28rev2%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Moon_jelly_-_adult_%28rev2%29.jpg/330px-Moon_jelly_-_adult_%28rev2%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Moon_jelly_-_adult_%28rev2%29.jpg/440px-Moon_jelly_-_adult_%28rev2%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moon_jelly_-_adult_(rev2).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An adult
        <a href="/wiki/Aurelia_(genus)" title="Aurelia (genus)">
         Aurelia
        </a>
        jellyfish which loggerheads eat during migration through the open sea
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The loggerhead sea turtle is omnivorous, feeding mainly on bottom-dwelling
      <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">
       invertebrates
      </a>
      , such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">
       gastropods
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bivalvia" title="Bivalvia">
       bivalves
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Decapoda" title="Decapoda">
       decapods
      </a>
      . It has a greater list of known prey than any other sea turtle. Other food items include
      <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">
       sponges
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">
       corals
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_pen" title="Sea pen">
       sea pens
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Polychaete" title="Polychaete">
       polychaete worms
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_anemone" title="Sea anemone">
       sea anemones
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">
       cephalopods
      </a>
      , barnacles,
      <a href="/wiki/Brachiopod" title="Brachiopod">
       brachiopods
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Isopoda" title="Isopoda">
       isopods
      </a>
      , insects,
      <a href="/wiki/Bryozoa" title="Bryozoa">
       bryozoans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_urchin" title="Sea urchin">
       sea urchins
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sand_dollar" title="Sand dollar">
       sand dollars
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_cucumber" title="Sea cucumber">
       sea cucumbers
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Starfish" title="Starfish">
       starfish
      </a>
      , fish (eggs, juveniles, and adults), hatchling turtles (including members of its own species), algae, and
      <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">
       vascular plants
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst50_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst50-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During migration through the open sea, loggerheads eat
      <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">
       jellyfish
      </a>
      , floating molluscs, floating egg clusters,
      <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">
       squid
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Flying_fish" title="Flying fish">
       flying fish
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-noaa-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Loggerheads crush prey with their large and powerful jaws.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_9-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-noaa-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst52_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst52-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Projecting scale points on the anterior margin of the forelimbs allow manipulation of the food. These points can be used as "pseudo-claws" to tear large pieces of food in the loggerhead's mouth. The loggerhead will turn its neck sideways to consume the torn food on the scale points.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst52_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst52-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Inward-pointing,
      <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">
       mucus
      </a>
      -covered
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/papilla" title="wiktionary:papilla">
       papillae
      </a>
      found in the fore region of the loggerhead's
      <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">
       esophagus
      </a>
      filter out foreign bodies, such as fish hooks. The next region of the esophagus is not papillated, with numerous mucosal folds. The digestion rate in loggerheads is temperature-dependent; it increases as temperature increases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst52_36-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst52-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Predators">
       Predators
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Predators">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:110127_Running_ghost_crab_O_ceratophtalma_prey_Loggerhead_hatchling_Gnaraloo_Bay_Rookery.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/110127_Running_ghost_crab_O_ceratophtalma_prey_Loggerhead_hatchling_Gnaraloo_Bay_Rookery.JPG/220px-110127_Running_ghost_crab_O_ceratophtalma_prey_Loggerhead_hatchling_Gnaraloo_Bay_Rookery.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/110127_Running_ghost_crab_O_ceratophtalma_prey_Loggerhead_hatchling_Gnaraloo_Bay_Rookery.JPG/330px-110127_Running_ghost_crab_O_ceratophtalma_prey_Loggerhead_hatchling_Gnaraloo_Bay_Rookery.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/110127_Running_ghost_crab_O_ceratophtalma_prey_Loggerhead_hatchling_Gnaraloo_Bay_Rookery.JPG/440px-110127_Running_ghost_crab_O_ceratophtalma_prey_Loggerhead_hatchling_Gnaraloo_Bay_Rookery.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:110127_Running_ghost_crab_O_ceratophtalma_prey_Loggerhead_hatchling_Gnaraloo_Bay_Rookery.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A horned ghost crab (
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Ocypode_ceratophthalma" title="Ocypode ceratophthalma">
          Ocypodeceratophthalma
         </a>
        </i>
        ) preying on a loggerhead hatchling in
        <a href="/wiki/Gnaraloo" title="Gnaraloo">
         Gnaraloo
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">
         Western Australia
        </a>
        .
        <a href="/wiki/Ghost_crab" title="Ghost crab">
         Ghost crabs
        </a>
        are one of the chief causes of egg and hatchling mortality in sea turtles.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
         <a href="#cite_note-37">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          37
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
         <a href="#cite_note-38">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          38
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W1_39-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-W1-39">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          39
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fox_up_a_tree.jpg">
        <img alt="A red fox walking along a fallen tree" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1998" data-file-width="3024" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Fox_up_a_tree.jpg/220px-Fox_up_a_tree.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Fox_up_a_tree.jpg/330px-Fox_up_a_tree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Fox_up_a_tree.jpg/440px-Fox_up_a_tree.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fox_up_a_tree.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">
         red fox
        </a>
        is a predator of loggerhead nests in Australia.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Loggerheads have numerous predators, especially early in their lives. Egg and nestling predators include
      <a href="/wiki/Ghost_crab" title="Ghost crab">
       ghost crabs
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oligochaete_worm" title="Oligochaete worm">
       oligochaete worms
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">
       beetles
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">
       fly
      </a>
      larvae,
      <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">
       ants
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Parasitoid_wasp" title="Parasitoid wasp">
       parasitoid wasp
      </a>
      larvae,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flesh_flies" title="Flesh flies">
       flesh flies
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">
       snakes
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">
       gulls
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corvid" title="Corvid">
       corvids
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Opossum" title="Opossum">
       opossums
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">
       bears
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">
       rats
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Armadillo" title="Armadillo">
       armadillos
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mustelidae" title="Mustelidae">
       mustelids
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Skunk" title="Skunk">
       skunks
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canids" title="Canids">
       canids
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Procyonidae" title="Procyonidae">
       procyonids
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Felidae" title="Felidae">
       cats
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">
       pigs
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">
       humans
      </a>
      . During their migration from their nests to the sea, hatchlings are preyed on by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diptera" title="Diptera">
       dipteran larvae
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Crab" title="Crab">
       crabs
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Toad" title="Toad">
       toads
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">
       lizards
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">
       snakes
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">
       seabirds
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Frigatebird" title="Frigatebird">
       frigatebirds
      </a>
      , and other assorted
      <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">
       birds
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">
       mammals
      </a>
      . In the ocean, predators of the loggerhead juveniles include fish, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Parrotfish" title="Parrotfish">
       parrotfish
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Moray_eel" title="Moray eel">
       moray eels
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Portunidae" title="Portunidae">
       portunid crabs
      </a>
      . Adults are more rarely attacked due to their large size, but may be preyed on by large
      <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">
       sharks
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">
       seals
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Killer_whale" title="Killer whale">
       killer whales
      </a>
      . Nesting females are attacked by flesh flies,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feral" title="Feral">
       feral
      </a>
      dogs, and humans. Salt marsh mosquitos can also pester nesting females.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst52_36-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst52-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst53_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst53-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In Australia, the introduction of the
      <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">
       red fox
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       Vulpes vulpes
      </i>
      ) by British settlers in the 19th century led to significant reductions in loggerhead sea turtle populations. In one coastal section in eastern Australia during the 1970s, predation of turtle eggs destroyed up to 95% of all clutches laid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila171_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila171-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Aggressive efforts to destroy foxes in the 1980s and 1990s has reduced this impact; however, it is estimated that it will be the year 2020 before populations will experience complete recovery from such dramatic losses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-council62_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-council62-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Along the southeastern coast of the United States, the
      <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">
       raccoon
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       Procyon lotor
      </i>
      ) is the most destructive predator of nesting sites. Mortality rates of nearly 100% of all clutches laid in a season have been recorded on some Florida beaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila171_41-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila171-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is attributed to an increase in raccoon populations, which have flourished in urban environments. Aggressive efforts to protect nesting sites by covering them with wire mesh has significantly reduced the impact of raccoon predation on loggerhead sea turtle eggs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-council62_42-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-council62-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bald_Head_Island" title="Bald Head Island">
       Bald Head Island
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">
       North Carolina
      </a>
      , wire mesh screens are used on every confirmed nest to prevent excavation by resident red foxes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A new concern with the steel cage technique is interference with the normal development of the nestlings' magnetic sense due to the use of ferrous wire,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which may disrupt the turtles' ability to navigate properly. Efforts are underway to find a nonmagnetic material that will prevent predators gnawing through the barrier.
     </p>
     <p>
      Up to 40% of nesting females around the world have wounds believed to come from shark attacks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst53_40-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst53-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Disease_and_parasites">
       Disease and parasites
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Disease and parasites">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Infectious bacteria such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas" title="Pseudomonas">
        Pseudomonas
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">
        Salmonella
       </a>
      </i>
      attack loggerhead hatchlings and eggs. Fungi such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Penicillium" title="Penicillium">
        Penicillium
       </a>
      </i>
      infect loggerhead sea turtle nests and cloacae.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst53_40-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst53-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Fibropapillomatosis" title="Fibropapillomatosis">
       Fibropapillomatosis
      </a>
      disease caused by a form of the
      <a href="/wiki/Herpesviridae" title="Herpesviridae">
       herpes-type
      </a>
      virus threatens loggerheads with internal and external tumors. These tumors disrupt essential behaviors and, if on the eyes, cause permanent blindness.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-threats_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-threats-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trematode" title="Trematode">
       Trematodes
      </a>
      of the family
      <a href="/wiki/Spirorchiidae" title="Spirorchiidae">
       Spirorchiidae
      </a>
      inhabit tissues throughout the body of the loggerhead, including vital organs, such as the heart and the brain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trematodes_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-trematodes-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Trematode infection can be highly debilitating. For example,
      <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">
       inflammatory
      </a>
      trematode
      <a href="/wiki/Lesion" title="Lesion">
       lesions
      </a>
      can cause
      <a href="/wiki/Endocarditis" title="Endocarditis">
       endocarditis
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Neurological_disorder" title="Neurological disorder">
       neurological disease
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trematodes_46-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-trematodes-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A
      <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">
       nematode
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Angiostoma_carettae" title="Angiostoma carettae">
        Angiostoma carettae
       </a>
      </i>
      , also infects loggerheads,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lungworm_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lungworm-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      causing
      <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">
       histologic
      </a>
      lesions in the
      <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">
       respiratory tract
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lungworm_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lungworm-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      More than 100 species of animals from 13
      <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">
       phyla
      </a>
      , as well as 37 kinds of algae, live on loggerheads' backs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila177_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila177-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">
       parasitic
      </a>
      organisms, which increase drag, offer no known benefit to the turtle, although the dulling effect of organisms on shell color may improve
      <a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">
       camouflage
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila177_48-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila177-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Life_history">
       Life history
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Life history">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">
       Early life
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Early life">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Babylogger.jpg">
        <img alt=" Loggerhead hatchling crawling through the sand" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="703" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Babylogger.jpg/170px-Babylogger.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Babylogger.jpg/255px-Babylogger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Babylogger.jpg/340px-Babylogger.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Babylogger.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A hatchling loggerhead sea turtle on its journey to the ocean
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Hatchlings range in color from light brown to almost black, lacking the adult's distinct yellows and reds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtleguide104_8-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-turtleguide104-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Upon hatching, they measure about 4.6cm (1.8in) and weigh about 20g (0.7oz).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_9-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-noaa-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The eggs are typically laid on the beach in an area above the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strandline" title="Strandline">
       high-tide line
      </a>
      . The eggs are laid near the water so the hatchlings can return to the sea.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation10_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation10-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The loggerhead's sex is dictated by the temperature of the underground nest. Incubation temperatures generally range from 26-32C (79-90F). Sea turtle eggs kept at a constant incubating temperature of 32C become females. Eggs incubating at 28C become males. An incubation temperature of 30C results in an equal ratio of male to female hatchlings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yntema1013_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-yntema1013-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hatchlings from eggs in the middle of the clutch tend to be the largest, grow the fastest, and be the most active during the first few days of sea life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila171_41-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila171-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After incubating for around 80 days, hatchlings dig through the sand to the surface,usually occurs at night, when darkness increases the chance of escaping predation and damage from extreme sand surface temperatures is reduced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation10_49-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation10-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hatchlings enter the ocean by navigating toward the brighter horizon created by the reflection of the moon and starlight off the water's surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artificiallighting_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-artificiallighting-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Hatchlings can lose up to 20% of their body mass due to evaporation of water as they journey from nest to ocean.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila21_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila21-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They initially use the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Undertow_(wave_action)" title="Undertow (wave action)">
       undertow
      </a>
      to push them five to 10 m away from the shore.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila21_52-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila21-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Once in the ocean, they swim for about 20 hours, bringing them far offshore.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtleguide104_8-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-turtleguide104-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An iron compound,
      <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">
       magnetite
      </a>
      , in their brains allows the turtles to perceive the
      <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">
       Earth's magnetic field
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila22_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila22-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      for navigation. Many hatchlings use
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sargassum" title="Sargassum">
        Sargassum
       </a>
      </i>
      in the open ocean as protection until they reach 45cm (18in).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtleguide104_8-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-turtleguide104-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hatchling loggerheads live in this
      <a href="/wiki/Pelagic_zone" title="Pelagic zone">
       pelagic environment
      </a>
      until they reach juvenile age, and then they migrate to nearshore waters.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtleguide104_8-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-turtleguide104-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Maturation">
       Maturation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Maturation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caretta_caretta_060417w2.jpg">
        <img alt=" Photo of a loggerhead swimming above a reef" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1296" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Caretta_caretta_060417w2.jpg/220px-Caretta_caretta_060417w2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Caretta_caretta_060417w2.jpg/330px-Caretta_caretta_060417w2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Caretta_caretta_060417w2.jpg/440px-Caretta_caretta_060417w2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caretta_caretta_060417w2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A mature loggerhead sea turtle
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      When ocean waters cool, loggerheads must migrate to warmer areas or
      <a href="/wiki/Hibernation" title="Hibernation">
       hibernate
      </a>
      to some degree. In the coldest months, they submerge for up to seven hours at a time, emerging for only seven minutes to breathe. Although outdone by
      <a href="/wiki/Terrapin" title="Terrapin">
       freshwater turtles
      </a>
      , these are among the longest recorded dives for any air-breathing marine vertebrate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-divetimes_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-divetimes-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During their seasonal migration, juvenile loggerheads have the ability to use both magnetic and visual cues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orientation_cues_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-orientation_cues-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When both aids are available, they are used in conjunction; if one aid is not available, the other suffices.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orientation_cues_55-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-orientation_cues-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The turtles swim at about 1.6km/h (0.9kn; 0.4m/s) during migration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Like all marine turtles, the loggerhead prepares for reproduction in its foraging area. This takes place several years before the loggerhead migrates to a mating area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation4_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation4-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Female loggerheads first reproduce at ages 2833 in Southeastern United States and Australia, and at ages 1730 in South Africa. Age at first reproduction in the Mediterranean, Oman, Japan, and Brazil are unknown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila16_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila16-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nesting loggerheads have a straight carapace length of 70109cm (2843in). Because of the large range, carapace length is not a reliable indicator of sexual maturity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation9_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation9-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Their estimated maximum lifespan is 4767 years in the wild.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ernst50_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ernst50-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">
       Reproduction
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Reproduction">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_turtle_track_Sanibel_Island_Florida.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2007" data-file-width="3018" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Loggerhead_turtle_track_Sanibel_Island_Florida.JPG/220px-Loggerhead_turtle_track_Sanibel_Island_Florida.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Loggerhead_turtle_track_Sanibel_Island_Florida.JPG/330px-Loggerhead_turtle_track_Sanibel_Island_Florida.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Loggerhead_turtle_track_Sanibel_Island_Florida.JPG/440px-Loggerhead_turtle_track_Sanibel_Island_Florida.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_turtle_track_Sanibel_Island_Florida.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Loggerhead turtle track on a beach
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Logger_eggs_01.jpg">
        <img alt="A female loggerhead sea turtle from the back, laying eggs into the hole it has dug" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="359" data-file-width="359" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Logger_eggs_01.jpg/220px-Logger_eggs_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Logger_eggs_01.jpg/330px-Logger_eggs_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/Logger_eggs_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Logger_eggs_01.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A loggerhead sea turtle laying eggs
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Female loggerheads first reproduce between the ages of 17 and 33,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila16_58-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila16-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and their mating period may last more than six weeks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation4_57-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation4-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They court their mates, but these behaviors have not been thoroughly examined.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation6_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation6-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Male forms of courtship behavior include nuzzling, biting, and head and flipper movements.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation6_60-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation6-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Studies suggest females produce
      <a href="/wiki/Cloaca" title="Cloaca">
       cloacal
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">
       pheromones
      </a>
      to indicate reproductive ability.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation6_60-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation6-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Before mating, the male approaches a female and attempts to mount her, while she resists. Next, the male and female begin to circle each other. If the male has competitors, the female may let the males struggle with each other. The winner then mounts the female; the male's curved claws usually damage the shoulders of the female's shell during this process. Other courting males bite the male while he is attempting to copulate, damaging his flippers and tail, possibly exposing bones. Such damage can cause the male to dismount and may require weeks to heal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation6_60-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation6-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While nesting, females produce an average of 3.9 egg clutches, and then become quiescent, producing no eggs for two to three years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation4_57-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation4-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila170_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila170-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Unlike other sea turtles, courtship and mating usually do not take place near the nesting beach, but rather along migration routes between feeding and breeding grounds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation6_60-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation6-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Recent evidence indicates
      <a href="/wiki/Ovulation" title="Ovulation">
       ovulation
      </a>
      in loggerheads is mating-induced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-induced_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-induced-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Through the act of mating, the female ovulates eggs which are fertilized by the male. This is unique, as mating-induced ovulation is rare outside of mammals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-induced_62-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-induced-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the Northern Hemisphere, loggerheads mate from late March to early June. The
      <a href="/wiki/Nesting_season" title="Nesting season">
       nesting season
      </a>
      is short, between May and August in the Northern Hemisphere and between October and March in the Southern Hemisphere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation9_59-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation9-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Loggerheads may display multiple paternity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paternity_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-paternity-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Multiple paternity is possible due to sperm storage. The female can store sperm from multiple males in her oviducts until ovulation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sperm_storage_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sperm_storage-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
