        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A single clutch may have as many as five fathers, each contributing sperm to a portion of the clutch.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paternity_63-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-paternity-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Multiple paternity and female size are
      <a href="/wiki/Correlation_and_dependence" title="Correlation and dependence">
       positively correlated
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paternity_63-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-paternity-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Two hypotheses explain this correlation. One posits that males favor large females because of their perceived higher
      <a href="/wiki/Fecundity" title="Fecundity">
       fecundity
      </a>
      (ability to reproduce).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paternity_63-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-paternity-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The other states, because larger females are able to swim more quickly to mating grounds, they have longer mating periods.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paternity_63-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-paternity-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      All sea turtles have similar basic nesting behaviors. Females return to lay eggs at intervals of 1217 days during the nesting season, on or near the beach where they hatched.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation6_60-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation6-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila170_61-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila170-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They exit the water, climb the beach, and scrape away the surface sand to form a body pit. With their hind limbs, they excavate an egg chamber in which the eggs are deposited. The females then cover the egg chamber and body pit with sand, and finally return to the sea.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation13_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation13-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This process takes one to two hours, and occurs in open sand areas or on top of sand dunes, preferably near dune grasses that the females can use to camouflage the nest.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila170_61-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila170-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The nesting area must be selected carefully because it affects characteristics such as fitness, emergence ratio, and vulnerability to nest predators.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation10_49-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation10-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Loggerheads have an average
      <a href="/wiki/Clutch_(eggs)" title="Clutch (eggs)">
       clutch
      </a>
      size of 112.4 eggs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation17_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation17-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">
       Taxonomy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolus_Linnaeus" title="Carolus Linnaeus">
       Carolus Linnaeus
      </a>
      gave the loggerhead its first binomial name,
      <i>
       Testudo caretta
      </i>
      , in 1758.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dodd1_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-dodd1-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seaturtlebook7_10-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-seaturtlebook7-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thirty-five other names emerged over the following two centuries, with the combination
      <i>
       Caretta caretta
      </i>
      first introduced in 1902 by Leonhard Stejneger.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dodd2_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-dodd2-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The English
      <a href="/wiki/Common_name" title="Common name">
       common name
      </a>
      "loggerhead" refers to the animal's large head.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_9-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-noaa-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dodd4_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dodd4-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The loggerhead sea turtle belongs to the
      <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">
       family
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Cheloniidae" title="Cheloniidae">
       Cheloniidae
      </a>
      , which includes all extant sea turtles except the
      <a href="/wiki/Leatherback_sea_turtle" title="Leatherback sea turtle">
       leatherback sea turtle
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtleguide97_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-turtleguide97-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The subspecific classification of the loggerhead sea turtle is debated, but most authors consider it a single
      <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">
       polymorphic
      </a>
      species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marquez14_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-marquez14-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Molecular genetics has confirmed hybridization of the loggerhead sea turtle with the
      <a href="/wiki/Kemp%27s_ridley_sea_turtle" title="Kemp's ridley sea turtle">
       Kemp's ridley sea turtle
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hawksbill_sea_turtle" title="Hawksbill sea turtle">
       hawksbill sea turtle
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Green_sea_turtle" title="Green sea turtle">
       green sea turtles
      </a>
      . The extent of natural hybridization is not yet determined; however, second-generation hybrids have been reported, suggesting some hybrids are fertile.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hybrid581_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hybrid581-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">
       Evolutionary history
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Evolutionary history">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Although evidence is lacking,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witherington12_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Witherington12-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      modern sea turtles probably descended from a single common ancestor during the Cretaceous period. Like all other sea turtles except the leatherback, loggerheads are members of the ancient
      <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">
       family
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Cheloniidae" title="Cheloniidae">
       Cheloniidae
      </a>
      , and appeared about 40 million years ago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila59_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila59-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Of the six species of living Cheloniidae, loggerheads are more closely related to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kemp%27s_Ridley" title="Kemp's Ridley">
       Kemp's ridley sea turtle
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Olive_ridley_sea_turtle" title="Olive ridley sea turtle">
       olive ridley sea turtle
      </a>
      , and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawksbill_turtle" title="Hawksbill turtle">
       hawksbill turtle
      </a>
      than they are to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flatback_turtle" title="Flatback turtle">
       flatback turtle
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_turtle" title="Green turtle">
       green turtle
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Around three million years ago, during the
      <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">
       Pliocene
      </a>
      epoch, Central America emerged from the sea, effectively cutting off currents between the Atlantic and
      <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific" title="Indo-Pacific">
       Indo-Pacific
      </a>
      Oceans. The rerouting of ocean currents led to climatic changes as the Earth entered a glacial cycle. Cold water upwelling around the
      <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">
       Cape of Good Hope
      </a>
      and reduction in water temperature at
      <a href="/wiki/Cape_Horn" title="Cape Horn">
       Cape Horn
      </a>
      formed coldwater barriers to migrating turtles. The result was a complete isolation of the Atlantic and Pacific populations of loggerheads.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila167_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila167-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During the most recent
      <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">
       ice age
      </a>
      , the beaches of southeastern North America were too cold for sea turtle eggs. As the Earth began to warm, loggerheads moved farther north, colonizing the northern beaches. Because of this, turtles nesting between North Carolina and northern Florida represent a different genetic population from those in southern Florida.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila167_72-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila167-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The distinct populations of loggerheads have unique characteristics and genetic differences. For example, Mediterranean loggerheads are smaller, on average, than Atlantic Ocean loggerheads.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila166_17-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila166-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      North Atlantic and Mediterranean loggerhead sea turtles are descendants of colonizing loggerheads from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tongaland" title="Tongaland">
       Tongaland
      </a>
      , South Africa. South African loggerhead genes are still present in these populations today.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila167_72-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila167-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">
       Conservation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Conservation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Many human activities have negative effects on loggerhead sea turtle populations. The prolonged time required for loggerheads to reach sexual maturity and the high mortality rates of eggs and young turtles from natural phenomena compound the problems of population reduction as a consequence of human activities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila178_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila178-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Threats">
       Threats
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Threats">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_nesting_area.jpg">
        <img alt=' An orange diamond sign with the words "Loggerhead Turtle Nesting Area" is blocking off a roped-off area on the beach where a loggerhead has laid eggs.' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="1296" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Loggerhead_nesting_area.jpg/220px-Loggerhead_nesting_area.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Loggerhead_nesting_area.jpg/330px-Loggerhead_nesting_area.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Loggerhead_nesting_area.jpg/440px-Loggerhead_nesting_area.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_nesting_area.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Loggerhead sea turtle nest roped off as part of the Sea Turtle Protection Project on
        <a href="/wiki/Hilton_Head_Island,_South_Carolina" title="Hilton Head Island, South Carolina">
         Hilton Head Island
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Loggerhead sea turtles were once intensively hunted for their meat and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">
       eggs
      </a>
      ; consumption has decreased, however, due to worldwide legislation. Despite this, turtle meat and eggs are still consumed in countries where regulations are not strictly enforced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eatingturtles_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-eatingturtles-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Mexico, turtle eggs are a common meal; locals claim the egg is an
      <a href="/wiki/Aphrodisiac" title="Aphrodisiac">
       aphrodisiac
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eatturtleeggs_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-eatturtleeggs-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Eating turtle eggs or meat can cause serious illness due to harmful bacteria, such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_aeruginosa" title="Pseudomonas aeruginosa">
        Pseudomonas aeruginosa
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Serratia_marcescens" title="Serratia marcescens">
        Serratia marcescens
       </a>
      </i>
      , and high levels of toxic metals that build up through
      <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">
       bioaccumulation
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eatingturtles_74-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-eatingturtles-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seaturtlebacteria_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-seaturtlebacteria-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The US West Coast is a critical migratory corridor for the Pacific loggerheads, in which these turtles swim across the Pacific to Californias coast from breeding grounds in Japan. Important foraging habitats for juveniles in the central North Pacific have been revealed through
      <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">
       telemetry
      </a>
      studies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plosone_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-plosone-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Along with these foraging habitats, high levels of bycatch from industrial-scale fisheries have been found to overlap; with drift gillnets in the past and longline fisheries presently.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plosone_77-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-plosone-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many juvenile loggerheads aggregate off the coast of Baja California Sur, Mexico, where small coastal fisheries increase these turtles' mortality risk; fishers have reported catching dozens of loggerheads with bottom-set gear per day per boat.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plosone_77-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-plosone-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most common commercial fishery that accidentally takes loggerheads are bottom trawls used for shrimp vessels in the Gulf of California.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMF_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NMF-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2000, between 2,600 and 6,000 loggerheads were estimated to have been killed by pelagic longlining in the Pacific.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewison_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lewison-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Fishing gear is the biggest threat to loggerheads in the open ocean. They often become entangled in
      <a href="/wiki/Longline_fishing" title="Longline fishing">
       longlines
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gillnet" title="Gillnet">
       gillnets
      </a>
      . According to the 2009 status review of loggerheads by the Fisheries Service, drowning from entanglement in longline and gillnet fishing gear is the turtles primary threat in the North Pacific.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMF_78-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NMF-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They also become stuck in traps, pots,
      <a href="/wiki/Fishing_trawler" title="Fishing trawler">
       trawls
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dredge" title="Dredge">
       dredges
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_9-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-noaa-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Caught in this unattended equipment, loggerheads risk serious injury or drowning. Turtle excluder devices for nets and other traps reduce the number being accidentally caught.
     </p>
     <p>
      Nearly 24,000 metric tons of plastic are dumped into the ocean each year. Turtles ingest a wide array of this floating debris, including bags, sheets, pellets,
      <a href="/wiki/Balloon_release" title="Balloon release">
       balloons
      </a>
      and abandoned fishing line.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-council114_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-council114-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Loggerheads may mistake the floating plastic for jellyfish, a common food item. The ingested plastic causes numerous health concerns, including intestinal blockage, reduced nutrient absorption and malnutrition, suffocation, ulcerations, or starvation. Ingested plastics release toxic compounds, including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyls" title="Polychlorinated biphenyls">
       polychlorinated biphenyls
      </a>
      , which may accumulate in internal tissues. Such toxins may lead to a thinning of eggshells, tissue damage, or deviation from natural behaviors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-council115_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-council115-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_light" title="Electric light">
       Artificial lighting
      </a>
      discourages nesting and interferes with the hatchlings' ability to navigate to the water's edge. Females prefer nesting on beaches free of artificial lighting. On developed beaches, nests are often clustered around tall buildings, perhaps because they block out the man-made light sources.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologyandconservation10_49-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-biologyandconservation10-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Loggerhead hatchlings are drawn toward the brighter area over the water which is the consequence of the reflection of moon and star light. Confused by the brighter artificial light, they navigate inland, away from the protective waters, which exposes them to dehydration and predation as the sun rises.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artificiallighting_51-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-artificiallighting-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Artificial lighting causes tens of thousands of hatchling deaths per year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lorne23_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lorne23-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Destruction and encroachment of habitat by humans is another threat to loggerhead sea turtles. Optimum nesting beaches are open-sand beaches above the high-tide line. However, beach development deprives them of suitable nesting areas, forcing them to nest closer to the surf.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila170_61-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila170-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Urbanization often leads to the
      <a href="/wiki/Siltation" title="Siltation">
       siltation
      </a>
      of sandy beaches, decreasing their viability.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila170_61-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila170-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Construction of docks and marinas can destroy near-shore habitats. Boat traffic and dredging degrades habitat and can also injure or kill turtles when boats collide with turtles at or near the surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-threats_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-threats-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Annual variations in climatic temperatures can affect sex ratios, since loggerheads have temperature-dependent sex determination. High sand temperatures may skew gender ratios in favor of females. Nesting sites exposed to unseasonably warm temperatures over a three-year period produced 8799% females.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This raises concern over the connection between rapid global temperature changes and the possibility of population extinction.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-janzen7489_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-janzen7489-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A more localized effect on gender skewing comes from the construction of tall buildings, which reduce sun exposure, lowering the average sand temperature, which results in a shift in gender ratios to favor the emergence of male turtles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotila170_61-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-spotila170-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_efforts">
       Conservation efforts
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Conservation efforts">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Logger_ted_01.jpg">
        <img alt="A loggerhead sea turtle escapes a circular fisherman's net via a TED" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="419" data-file-width="514" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Logger_ted_01.jpg/220px-Logger_ted_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Logger_ted_01.jpg/330px-Logger_ted_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Logger_ted_01.jpg/440px-Logger_ted_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Logger_ted_01.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Loggerhead sea turtle escapes from fishing net through a
        <a href="/wiki/Turtle_excluder_device" title="Turtle excluder device">
         turtle excluder device
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Since the loggerhead occupies such a broad range, successful conservation requires efforts from multiple countries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_9-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-noaa-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Loggerhead sea turtles are classified as endangered by the International Union for the Conservation of Nature and are listed under Appendix I of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_on_International_Trade_in_Endangered_Species" title="Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species">
       Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species
      </a>
      , making international trade illegal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_9-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-noaa-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the United States, the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Fish_and_Wildlife_Service" title="United States Fish and Wildlife Service">
       Fish and Wildlife Service
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/National_Marine_Fisheries_Service" title="National Marine Fisheries Service">
       National Marine Fisheries Service
      </a>
      classify them as a threatened species under the
      <a href="/wiki/Endangered_Species_Act" title="Endangered Species Act">
       Endangered Species Act
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_9-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-noaa-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Loggerheads are listed as endangered under both Australia's
      <a href="/wiki/Environment_Protection_and_Biodiversity_Conservation_Act_1999" title="Environment Protection and Biodiversity Conservation Act 1999">
       Environment Protection and Biodiversity Conservation Act 1999
      </a>
      and Queensland's
      <a href="/wiki/Nature_Conservation_Act_1992" title="Nature Conservation Act 1992">
       Nature Conservation Act 1992
      </a>
      . The Convention on Migratory Species works for the conservation of loggerhead sea turtles on the Atlantic coast of Africa, as well as in the Indian Ocean and southeast Asia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-southafricaconservation_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-southafricaconservation-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-southeastasiaconservation_86-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-southeastasiaconservation-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Throughout Japan, the Sea Turtle Association of Japan aids in the conservation of loggerhead sea turtles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-japanconservation_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-japanconservation-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Greece's
      <a href="/wiki/ARCHELON,_the_Sea_Turtle_Protection_Society_of_Greece" title="ARCHELON, the Sea Turtle Protection Society of Greece">
       ARCHELON
      </a>
      works for their conservation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greececonservation_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-greececonservation-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Marine Research Foundation works for loggerhead conservation in Oman.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omanconservation_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-omanconservation-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Annex 2 of the Specially Protected Areas and Wildlife Protocol of the Cartagena Convention, which deals with pollution that could harm marine ecosystems, also protects them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_9-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-noaa-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cartagenaconvention_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cartagenaconvention-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Conservation organizations worldwide have worked with the shrimp trawling industry to develop turtle exclusion devices (TEDs) to exclude even the largest turtles. TEDs are mandatory for all shrimp trawlers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_9-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-noaa-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_SeaTag-GEO.jpg">
        <img alt="A loggerhead sea turtle is resting on the beach, with an antenna attached to its back." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Loggerhead_SeaTag-GEO.jpg/220px-Loggerhead_SeaTag-GEO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Loggerhead_SeaTag-GEO.jpg/330px-Loggerhead_SeaTag-GEO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Loggerhead_SeaTag-GEO.jpg/440px-Loggerhead_SeaTag-GEO.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Loggerhead_SeaTag-GEO.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A loggerhead sea turtle, Evelyn, is equipped with a satellite tag and a SeaTag-GEO solar-powered
        <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">
         geomagnetic
        </a>
        tag
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In many places during the nesting season, workers and volunteers search the coastline for nests,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-volunteerwork_91-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-volunteerwork-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and researchers may also go out during the evening to look for nesting females for tagging studies and gather barnacles and tissues samples. Volunteers may, if necessary, relocate the nests for protection from threats, such as high spring tides and predators, and monitor the nests daily for disturbances. After the eggs hatch, volunteers uncover and tally hatched eggs, undeveloped eggs, and dead hatchlings. Any remaining live hatchlings are released or taken to research facilities. Typically, those that lack the vitality to hatch and climb to the surface die.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seaturtlebook13_92-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-seaturtlebook13-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hatchlings use the journey from nest to ocean to build strength for the coming swim. Helping them to reach the ocean bypasses this strength-building exercise and lowers their chances of survival.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitmeyer_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Whitmeyer-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">
       Symbols
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Symbols">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The loggerhead sea turtle is the official
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_reptile" title="State reptile">
       state reptile
      </a>
      of South Carolina and also the state saltwater reptile of Florida.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Florida_94-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Florida-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shearer323_95-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shearer323-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
       See also
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Loggerhead_sea_turtle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: See also">

       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

       </a>

       </a>
       .

          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">

          </span>
          .
          <q>
           This project aimed to continue to build on Omans programme to conduct surveys, develop survey protocols and provide equipment and material and personnel support for Government rangers
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALoggerhead+sea+turtle&amp;rft.atitle=Ongoing+Conservation+Initiatives&amp;rft.aulast=Marine+Research+Foundation&amp;rft.au=Marine+Research+Foundation&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fmrf-asia.org%2Fprojects_view.cfm%3FTHE_ID%3D34&amp;rft.jtitle=Marine+Research+Foundation&amp;rft.pub=Marine+Research+Foundation&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal">

          <q>
           The Cartagena Convention requires Parties to adopt measures aimed at preventing, reducing and controlling pollution of the following areas: pollution from ships; pollution caused by dumping; pollution from sea-bed activities; airborne pollution; and pollution from land-based sources and activities.
          </q>

       <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALoggerhead+sea+turtle&amp;rft.aufirst=Peter+L.&amp;rft.aulast=Lutz&amp;rft.au=Lutz%2C+Peter+L.&amp;rft.au=Musick%2C+John+A.&amp;rft.au=Wyneken%2C+Jeanette&amp;rft.btitle=The+Biology+of+Sea+Turtles&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-8493-8422-2&amp;rft.place=Boca+Raton%2C+Florida&amp;rft.pub=CRC+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.volume=1">
        <span style="display:none;">
        </span>
       </span>
      </li>
